<p><b>Valve Corporation</b> is an American <a href="/wiki/Video_game_developer" title="Video game developer">video game developer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Video_game_publisher" title="Video game publisher">publisher</a>  and <a href="/wiki/Digital_distribution" title="Digital distribution">digital distribution</a> company headquartered in <a href="/wiki/Bellevue,_Washington" title="Bellevue, Washington">Bellevue</a>, <a href="/wiki/Washington_(state)" title="Washington (state)">Washington</a>. It is the developer of the software distribution platform <a href="/wiki/Steam_(software)" title="Steam (software)">Steam</a> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Half-Life_(series)" title="Half-Life (series)">Half-Life</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Counter-Strike" title="Counter-Strike">Counter-Strike</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Portal_(video_game_series)" class="mw-redirect" title="Portal (video game series)">Portal</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Day_of_Defeat" title="Day of Defeat">Day of Defeat</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Team_Fortress" class="mw-redirect" title="Team Fortress">Team Fortress</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Left_4_Dead_(series)" title="Left 4 Dead (series)">Left 4 Dead</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Dota_2" title="Dota 2">Dota 2</a></i> games.
</p><p>Valve was founded in 1996 by former <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a> employees <a href="/wiki/Gabe_Newell" title="Gabe Newell">Gabe Newell</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mike_Harrington" title="Mike Harrington">Mike Harrington</a>. Their debut product, the PC <a href="/wiki/First-person_shooter" title="First-person shooter">first-person shooter</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Half-Life_(series)" title="Half-Life (series)">Half-Life</a></i>, was released in 1998 to critical acclaim and commercial success, after which Harrington left the company. In 2003, Valve launched Steam, which accounted for around half of digital PC game sales by 2011. By 2012, Valve employed around 250 people and was reportedly worth over <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$</span>3 billion, making it the most profitable company per employee in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> In 2015, Valve entered the game hardware market with the <a href="/wiki/Steam_Machine_(hardware_platform)" title="Steam Machine (hardware platform)">Steam Machine</a>, a line of third-party built <a href="/wiki/Gaming_PC" class="mw-redirect" title="Gaming PC">gaming PCs</a> running Valve's <a href="/wiki/SteamOS" title="SteamOS">SteamOS</a> <a href="/wiki/Operating_system" title="Operating system">operating system</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Founding">Founding</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Valve_old_logo.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Valve_old_logo.svg/220px-Valve_old_logo.svg.png" width="220" height="60" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Valve_old_logo.svg/330px-Valve_old_logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Valve_old_logo.svg/440px-Valve_old_logo.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1098" data-file-height="300" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Valve_old_logo.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Valve's logo between 1996 and 2018</div></div></div>
<p>Valve was founded by former longtime <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a> employees <a href="/wiki/Gabe_Newell" title="Gabe Newell">Gabe Newell</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mike_Harrington" title="Mike Harrington">Mike Harrington</a> on August 24, 1996,<sup id="cite_ref-:0_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> as Valve, L.L.C., based in <a href="/wiki/Kirkland,_Washington" title="Kirkland, Washington">Kirkland, Washington</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-hist_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hist-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Alternative names explored by Newell and Harrington include "Fruitfly Ensemble" and "Rhino Scar".<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Harrington left the company in 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-hist_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hist-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> In 2003, the company moved from its original location to <a href="/wiki/Bellevue,_Washington" title="Bellevue, Washington">Bellevue, Washington</a>, and re-incorporated as Valve Corporation.<sup id="cite_ref-hist_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hist-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> In 2010, the office was moved again to a larger location in Bellevue. In 2016, Valve signed a nine-floor lease in the <a href="/wiki/Lincoln_Square_(Bellevue)" title="Lincoln Square (Bellevue)">Lincoln Square</a> complex in downtown Bellevue, doubling the size of their offices.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Half-Life"><i>Half-Life</i></span></h3>
<p>For its first product, Valve settled on a concept for a horror <a href="/wiki/First-person_shooter" title="First-person shooter">first-person shooter</a> (FPS) using the <a href="/wiki/Quake_engine" title="Quake engine"><i>Quake</i> engine</a> licensed from <a href="/wiki/Id_Software" title="Id Software">id Software</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-flhalflife22_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-flhalflife22-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Half-Life_(video_game)" title="Half-Life (video game)">Half-Life</a></i> was released in November 1998.<sup id="cite_ref-fhhalflife5_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fhhalflife5-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> It was praised by numerous publications as one of the best and most influential games of all time;<sup id="cite_ref-cgw_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cgw-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gamespotrev_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gamespotrev-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-gamespotfame_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gamespotfame-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> according to IGN, the history of the FPS genre "breaks down pretty cleanly into pre-<i>Half-Life</i> and post-<i>Half-Life</i> eras."<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Valve acquired TF Software Pty. Ltd., the makers of the <i><a href="/wiki/Team_Fortress" class="mw-redirect" title="Team Fortress">Team Fortress</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Mod_(computer_gaming)" class="mw-redirect" title="Mod (computer gaming)">mod</a> for <i><a href="/wiki/Quake_(video_game)" title="Quake (video game)">Quake</a></i>, in May 1998 with the intent to create a standalone <i>Team Fortress</i> game.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Team_Fortress_Classic" title="Team Fortress Classic">Team Fortress Classic</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Mod_(computer_gaming)" class="mw-redirect" title="Mod (computer gaming)">mod</a>, a port of the original <i>Team Fortress</i> mod for <i>Quake</i>, was released for <i>Half-Life</i> in 1999.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Gearbox_Software" title="Gearbox Software">Gearbox Software</a> created the expansion packs <i><a href="/wiki/Half-Life:_Opposing_Force" title="Half-Life: Opposing Force">Opposing Force</a>,</i> <i><a href="/wiki/Half-Life:_Blue_Shift" title="Half-Life: Blue Shift">Blue Shift</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Half-Life:_Decay" title="Half-Life: Decay">Decay</a>,</i> and ported the game to <a href="/wiki/PlayStation_2" title="PlayStation 2">PlayStation 2</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> A port to <a href="/wiki/Dreamcast" title="Dreamcast">Dreamcast</a> was canceled in 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Source">Source</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Source_(game_engine)" title="Source (game engine)">Source (game engine)</a></div>
<p>After the success of <i>Half-Life</i>, the team worked on mods, <a href="/wiki/Spin-off_(media)" title="Spin-off (media)">spin-offs</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sequel" title="Sequel">sequels</a>, including <i><a href="/wiki/Half-Life_2" title="Half-Life 2">Half-Life 2</a></i>. All current Valve games are built on its <a href="/wiki/Source_(game_engine)" title="Source (game engine)">Source</a> engine. The company has developed six game series: <i>Half-Life</i>, <i>Team Fortress</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Portal_(video_game)" title="Portal (video game)">Portal</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Counter-Strike" title="Counter-Strike">Counter-Strike</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Left_4_Dead" title="Left 4 Dead">Left 4 Dead</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Day_of_Defeat" title="Day of Defeat">Day of Defeat</a></i>. Valve is noted for its support of its games' modding community, most prominently, <i>Counter-Strike</i>, <i>Team Fortress</i>, and <i>Day of Defeat</i>. Valve has branched out with this tradition to continue developing <i><a href="/wiki/Dota_2" title="Dota 2">Dota 2</a></i> as the standalone sequel to the <i><a href="/wiki/Warcraft_III:_Reign_of_Chaos" title="Warcraft III: Reign of Chaos">Warcraft III</a></i> mod.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Each of these games began as a third-party mod that Valve purchased and developed into a full game. They also distribute community mods on <a href="/wiki/Steam_(software)" title="Steam (software)">Steam</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-sfm_home_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sfm_home-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Valve announced the <a href="/wiki/Source_(game_engine)#Source_2" title="Source (game engine)">Source 2</a> engine in March 2015, later porting the entirety of <i>Dota 2</i> to the engine in September of that year.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Acquisitions_and_awards">Acquisitions and awards</span></h3>
<p>Valve has grown both in scope and commercial value. In January 2008, they announced the acquisition of <a href="/wiki/Turtle_Rock_Studios" title="Turtle Rock Studios">Turtle Rock Studios</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Valve_2008-01-10_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Valve_2008-01-10-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> which would be renamed Valve South.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Turtle Rock Studios spun out of Valve again in March 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> In April 2010, the company won <i><a href="/wiki/The_Escapist_Magazine" class="mw-redirect" title="The Escapist Magazine">The Escapist Magazine</a></i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;s</span> March Mayhem tournament for the best developer of 2010, beating out <i><a href="/wiki/Zynga" title="Zynga">Zynga</a></i> in the semi-finals and <i><a href="/wiki/BioWare" title="BioWare">BioWare</a></i> in the final.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In December 2012, Valve acquired Star Filled Studios, a two-man gaming company, to open a San Francisco office.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> However, Valve ended the operation in August 2013 when it was decided that there was little benefit coming from the arrangement.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> In April 2018, Valve acquired the independent developer <a href="/wiki/Campo_Santo_(company)" title="Campo Santo (company)">Campo Santo</a>, known for the 2016 adventure game <i><a href="/wiki/Firewatch" title="Firewatch">Firewatch</a></i>. Campo Santo will continue to develop its own titles under Valve.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Network_intrusions">Network intrusions</span></h3>
<p>Valve's internal network has been infiltrated by hackers three times, in 2003 where content of the yet to be released <i>Half-Life 2</i> was leaked onto the internet,<sup id="cite_ref-source_code_leak_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-source_code_leak-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Newell's email account was compromised, and <a href="/wiki/Keystroke_logging" title="Keystroke logging">keyloggers</a> were installed on several Valve systems.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> In 2011 the Steam customer databases and forums were compromised.<sup id="cite_ref-arssteamhack_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-arssteamhack-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-registerhack_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-registerhack-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> In September 2011, a hacker broke into the network and downloaded the beta code of <i><a href="/wiki/Call_of_Duty:_Modern_Warfare_3" title="Call of Duty: Modern Warfare 3">Call of Duty: Modern Warfare 3</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In June 2014, a developer from <a href="/wiki/SCS_Software" title="SCS Software">SCS Software</a> reported an exploit that allowed announcement pages to be injected with code, and after no response, he edited an announcement to redirect users to a <a href="/wiki/Harlem_Shake_(meme)" title="Harlem Shake (meme)">Harlem Shake</a> video.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> In March 2016, a vulnerability on the Steam Store allowed a user to publish a game without any authorization from Valve.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Games">Games</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Valve_Corporation_video_games" title="List of Valve Corporation video games">List of Valve Corporation video games</a></div>
<p>Valve has developed and published the main games in both the <i><a href="/wiki/Half-Life_(series)" title="Half-Life (series)">Half-Life</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Portal_(video_game)" title="Portal (video game)">Portal</a></i> series, as well as published both and developed one of the <i><a href="/wiki/Left_4_Dead" title="Left 4 Dead">Left 4 Dead</a></i> games, the other of which was developed by Valve South (now <a href="/wiki/Turtle_Rock_Studios" title="Turtle Rock Studios">Turtle Rock Studios</a>). Valve also developed and published <i><a href="/wiki/Team_Fortress" class="mw-redirect" title="Team Fortress">Team Fortress</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Team_Fortress_2" title="Team Fortress 2">Team Fortress 2</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Dota_2" title="Dota 2">Dota 2</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Artifact_(video_game)" title="Artifact (video game)">Artifact</a></i>.
</p><p>Several of Valve's series feature only two primary games, such as <i>Half-Life</i> and <i>Half-Life 2</i>. With no apparent announcements of a third title in these series, Valve has acquired a joking reputation for being unable to count to 3.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> In the absence of an official announcement of a <i>Half-Life 3</i>, players and journalists have repeatedly claimed to have found proof that a sequel remained under active development, many of which have been revealed as hoaxes or leaks of dubious authenticity.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Unreleased and cancelled games include a fairy-themed <a href="/wiki/Role-playing_video_game" title="Role-playing video game">role-playing game</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> <i>Prospero,</i><sup id="cite_ref-raisingbar_prospero_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-raisingbar_prospero-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> and <i>Stars of Blood</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Valve worked with <a href="/wiki/Arkane_Studios" title="Arkane Studios">Arkane Studios</a> on <i><a href="/wiki/The_Crossing_(video_game)" title="The Crossing (video game)">The Crossing</a></i>, which was canceled in May 2009. Arkane tried to develop <i>Return to Ravenholm</i> without consent by Valve, which was also canceled.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Steam">Steam</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Steam_(software)" title="Steam (software)">Steam (software)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gabe_newell_doug_lombardi.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Gabe_newell_doug_lombardi.jpg/220px-Gabe_newell_doug_lombardi.jpg" width="220" height="233" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Gabe_newell_doug_lombardi.jpg/330px-Gabe_newell_doug_lombardi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Gabe_newell_doug_lombardi.jpg/440px-Gabe_newell_doug_lombardi.jpg 2x" data-file-width="467" data-file-height="495" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gabe_newell_doug_lombardi.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Gabe_Newell" title="Gabe Newell">Gabe Newell</a> (foreground) and Doug Lombardi (background), 2007</div></div></div>
<p>Valve announced Steam, its digital distribution software platform, at the 2002 <a href="/wiki/Game_Developers_Conference" title="Game Developers Conference">Game Developers Conference</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> It launched in September 2003 and was first used to deliver <a href="/wiki/Patch_(computing)" title="Patch (computing)">patches</a> and other updates to Valve's online games, which it later became mandatory to use.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On August 1, 2012, Valve announced revisions to the Steam Subscriber Agreement (SSA) to prohibit <a href="/wiki/Class_action" title="Class action">class action</a> <a href="/wiki/Lawsuit" title="Lawsuit">lawsuits</a> by users against the service provider.<sup id="cite_ref-augustrockpapershotgun_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-augustrockpapershotgun-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-valveaugustssa_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-valveaugustssa-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> By July 2014, there were over 3,400 games available on Steam, with over 150 million registered accounts by January 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-steamgrowth_2014_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-steamgrowth_2014-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Alongside these changes to the SSA, the company also declared publicly the incorporation of Valve <a href="/wiki/Soci%C3%A9t%C3%A9_%C3%A0_responsabilit%C3%A9_limit%C3%A9e" title="Société à responsabilité limitée">S.a.r.l.</a>, a subsidiary based in <a href="/wiki/Luxembourg" title="Luxembourg">Luxembourg</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-augustrockpapershotgun_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-augustrockpapershotgun-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-valveaugustssa_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-valveaugustssa-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Valve set up a physical office in Luxembourg Kirchberg. According to Valve's project manager Mike Dunkle, the location was chosen for eCommerce capabilities and infrastructure, talent acquisition, tax advantages and central geographic location&#160;– most major partners are accessible, with 50% within driving distance.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Valve S.a.r.l. was used to sell games to United Kingdom–based users to avoid paying the full 20% <a href="/wiki/Value-added_tax" title="Value-added tax">value-added tax</a> (VAT).<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Tax_avoidance" title="Tax avoidance">tax loophole</a> was expected to be closed on January 1, 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> In December 2015, the French consumer group <a href="/wiki/UFC_Que_Choisir" title="UFC Que Choisir">UFC Que Choisir</a> initiated a lawsuit against Valve for several of their Steam policies that conflict or run afoul of French law. One of the reasons was for using the tax loophole.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Valve S.a.r.l. stopped doing business on January 1, 2017, with the main company taking over EU sales again.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> In August 2017, Valve announced that Steam had reached over 67 million monthly and 33 million <a href="/wiki/Daily_active_users" title="Daily active users">daily active users</a> on the platform.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_projects">Other projects</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="PowerPlay">PowerPlay</span></h3>
<p>PowerPlay was a technological initiative headed by Valve and <a href="/wiki/Cisco_Systems" title="Cisco Systems">Cisco Systems</a> to decrease the latency for online computer games.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Gabe Newell, the managing director of Valve, announced the project in January 2000 and after 12 months the project was quietly abandoned.
</p><p>PowerPlay was described as a set of protocols and deployment standards at the <a href="/wiki/Router_(computing)" title="Router (computing)">router</a> level to improve performance. It was claimed that a player with 1000&#160;ms <a href="/wiki/Ping_(networking_utility)" title="Ping (networking utility)">ping</a> was able to play against another player on a <a href="/wiki/Local_area_network" title="Local area network">LAN</a> connection with no noticeable disadvantage.<sup id="cite_ref-planetfortress_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-planetfortress-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Initially the protocol was to be released with PowerPlay 1.0 focusing on <a href="/wiki/Quality_of_Service" class="mw-redirect" title="Quality of Service">Quality of Service</a> (QoS) and later a revision, PowerPlay 2.0 that would focus on functionality. Cisco and Valve intended to deliver a single dial-up service in Q1 2000 in the United States with a 30-day free trial with a bundled copy of <i><a href="/wiki/Team_Fortress" class="mw-redirect" title="Team Fortress">Team Fortress</a></i> modified to support PowerPlay.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Despite never deploying the dial-up plan featuring PowerPlay 1.0, Valve announced in January 2001 that the standard had indeed been finalized.<sup id="cite_ref-planetfortress_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-planetfortress-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The standard was to involve purchasing PowerPlay approved Cisco hardware and infrastructure that had adequate bandwidth and QoS standards that prioritize PowerPlay gaming packets at the expense of all others. <a href="/wiki/Gabe_Newell" title="Gabe Newell">Gabe Newell</a> conceded that <a href="/wiki/Internet_service_provider" title="Internet service provider">Internet service providers</a> (ISPs) would bear the brunt of this expense: "The ISPs are going to need to spend a fair amount of money to be compliant with PowerPlay. But how they get that back is up to them. Some will have a tiered service, and some will just try to recoup their investment through reduced customer churn and customer acquisition."<sup id="cite_ref-eurogamer1_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eurogamer1-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Steam_Machine">Steam Machine</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Steam_Machine_(hardware_platform)" title="Steam Machine (hardware platform)">Steam Machine (hardware platform)</a></div>
<p>Newell has been critical of the direction that Microsoft has taken with the Windows operating system in making it a <a href="/wiki/Open_architecture" title="Open architecture">closed architecture</a> similar to Apple's products, and has stated that he believes that the changes made in Windows 8 are "a catastrophe for everyone in the PC space".<sup id="cite_ref-ign_windows_8_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ign_windows_8-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Newell identified the open-source Linux platform as an ideal platform for Steam, noting that the only thing holding back its adoption is the lack of games.<sup id="cite_ref-ign_windows_8_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ign_windows_8-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2012, Valve announced that they were working on a console/PC hybrid for the living room which was unofficially dubbed by media as the "Steam Box".<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> A precursor to such a unit is SteamOS, a freely available Linux-based operating system that builds upon the Steam client functionality that includes media services, live streaming across home networks, game sharing within families, and parental controls. SteamOS was officially announced in September 2013 as the first of several announcements related to the Steam Machine platform<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> as well as their unique game controller.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> In May 2014, Valve announced that the company's own SteamOS-powered Steam Machine would be delayed until 2015 due to problems with the game controller.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> In 2015, <a href="/wiki/Alienware" title="Alienware">Alienware</a>, <a href="/wiki/ZOTAC" title="ZOTAC">ZOTAC</a>, and <a href="/wiki/CyberPowerPC" title="CyberPowerPC">CyberPowerPC</a> launched their versions of the Steam Machine. By June 2016, fewer than half a million had been sold.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pipeline">Pipeline</span></h3>
<p>In July 2013, Valve announced Pipeline, an <a href="/wiki/Internship" title="Internship">intern</a> project consisting of ten high school students working together to learn how to create video game content.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Pipeline serves to discuss and answer questions that teenagers often ask about the video game industry,<sup id="cite_ref-pipeline_home_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pipeline_home-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> and see if it is possible to train a group of teenagers with minimal work experience to work for a company like Valve.<sup id="cite_ref-pipeline_home_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pipeline_home-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> The latter purpose breaks Valve's tradition of employing experienced developers, as the company is not good at "teaching people straight out of school".<sup id="cite_ref-pipeline_home_77-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pipeline_home-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="J._J._Abrams_collaboration">J. J. Abrams collaboration</span></h3>
<p>At the 2013 <a href="/wiki/D.I.C.E._Summit" class="mw-redirect" title="D.I.C.E. Summit">D.I.C.E. Summit</a>, Gabe Newell confirmed that he and director <a href="/wiki/J._J._Abrams" title="J. J. Abrams">J. J. Abrams</a> were collaborating to produce a <i>Half-Life</i> or <i>Portal</i> film, as well as a possible new game.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="HTC_Vive">HTC Vive</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/HTC_Vive" title="HTC Vive">HTC Vive</a></div>
<p>In March 2015, Valve and Taiwanese electronics company <a href="/wiki/HTC" title="HTC">HTC</a> announced a joint project to develop the <a href="/wiki/HTC_Vive" title="HTC Vive">Vive</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Virtual_reality_headset" title="Virtual reality headset">virtual reality headset</a> with motion tracked controllers.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> The companies are working with <a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">Google</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lions_Gate" class="mw-redirect" title="Lions Gate">Lions Gate</a>, and <a href="/wiki/HBO" title="HBO">HBO</a> to develop content for the device.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Finances">Finances</span></h2>
<p>Valve does not make its finances public. In 2005, <i><a href="/wiki/Forbes" title="Forbes">Forbes</a></i> estimated that Valve had grossed $70 million that year. Ed Barton, a <a href="/wiki/Screen_Digest" title="Screen Digest">Screen Digest</a> analyst, estimated Valve's 2010 revenue to be in the "high hundreds of millions of dollars". As of  2011<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Valve_Corporation&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, the company had an estimated worth of $2 to 4 billion, and according to Newell, it was the most profitable company per employee in the United States. Most of Valve's revenue comes from Steam, which controlled 50 to 70% of the market for downloaded PC games in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Organizational_structure">Organizational structure</span></h2>
<p>Valve's early structure was similar to other development firms; this was principally driven by the nature of physical game releases through publishers that required tasks to be completed by given deadlines, requiring a more regimented structure.<sup id="cite_ref-gamasutra_newell_2017_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gamasutra_newell_2017-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> As the company moved to digital releases where they serve as their own publisher, the structure has become a <a href="/wiki/Flat_organization" title="Flat organization">flat organization</a>. Valve publicly published their employee handbook in 2012,<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-valve_neh_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-valve_neh-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> demonstrating at that time that outside of executive management, there were no bosses, and the company used an <a href="/wiki/Open_allocation" title="Open allocation">open allocation</a> system, allowing employees to move between departments at will.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> This approach allows employees to work on whatever interests them, but requires them to take ownership of their product and mistakes they may make, according to Newell. Newell recognized that this structure works well for some but that "there are plenty of great developers for whom this is a terrible place to work".<sup id="cite_ref-gamasutra_newell_2017_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gamasutra_newell_2017-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Economist <a href="/wiki/Yanis_Varoufakis" title="Yanis Varoufakis">Yanis Varoufakis</a>, the former <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Finance_(Greece)" title="Ministry of Finance (Greece)">Finance Minister</a> of <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>, worked as an economic consultant for Valve; he attempted to place Valve's organization in the context of <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_the_firm" title="Theory of the firm">theories of the firm</a> and broader economic thinking.<sup id="cite_ref-layoffs_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-layoffs-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-yanis_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yanis-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Former employee <a href="/wiki/Jeri_Ellsworth" title="Jeri Ellsworth">Jeri Ellsworth</a> criticized the structure as "a lot like high school" in that certain employees have more influence than others.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legal_disputes">Legal disputes</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Valve_Corporation_v._Vivendi_Universal_Games"><i>Valve Corporation v. Vivendi Universal Games</i></span></h3>
<p>Between 2002 and 2005, Valve was involved in a complex legal dispute with its publisher, <a href="/wiki/Vivendi_Universal_Games" class="mw-redirect" title="Vivendi Universal Games">Vivendi Universal Games</a> (under Vivendi's brand <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Entertainment" title="Sierra Entertainment">Sierra Entertainment</a>). It officially began on August 14, 2002, when Valve sued Sierra for <a href="/wiki/Copyright_infringement" title="Copyright infringement">copyright infringement</a>, alleging that the publisher had illegally distributed copies of their games to <a href="/wiki/Internet_cafe" class="mw-redirect" title="Internet cafe">Internet cafes</a>. They later added claims of <a href="/wiki/Breach_of_contract" title="Breach of contract">breach of contract</a>, accusing their publisher of withholding royalties and delaying the release of <i><a href="/wiki/Counter-Strike:_Condition_Zero" title="Counter-Strike: Condition Zero">Counter-Strike: Condition Zero</a></i> until after the holiday season.
</p><p>Vivendi fought back, saying that Gabe Newell and marketing director Doug Lombardi had misrepresented Valve's position in meetings with the publisher. Vivendi later countersued, claiming that Valve's Steam content distribution system attempted to circumvent their publishing agreement. Vivendi sought <a href="/wiki/Intellectual_property" title="Intellectual property">intellectual property</a> rights to <i>Half-Life</i> and a ruling preventing Valve from using Steam to distribute <i>Half-Life 2</i>.
</p><p>On November 29, 2004, Judge <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Samuel_Zilly" title="Thomas Samuel Zilly">Thomas Samuel Zilly</a> of the <a href="/wiki/U.S._District_Court_for_the_Western_District_of_Washington" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. District Court for the Western District of Washington">U.S. District Court for the Western District of Washington</a> ruled in favor of Valve. Specifically, the ruling stated that Vivendi Universal and its affiliates (including Sierra) were not authorized to distribute Valve games, either directly or indirectly, through cyber cafés to end users for <a href="/wiki/Pay-to-play" class="mw-redirect" title="Pay-to-play">pay-to-play</a> activities pursuant to the parties' publishing agreement. In addition, Judge Zilly ruled that Valve could recover copyright damages for infringements without regard to the publishing agreement's limitation of liability clause.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> Valve posted on the Steam website that the two companies had come to a settlement in court on April 29, 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Electronic_Arts" title="Electronic Arts">Electronic Arts</a> announced on July 18, 2005, that they would be teaming up with Valve in a multi-year deal to distribute their games, replacing Vivendi Universal from then onwards.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> As a result of the trial, the arbitrator also awarded Valve $2,391,932.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Valve_Corporation_v._Activision_Blizzard"><i>Valve Corporation v. Activision Blizzard</i></span></h3>
<p>In April 2009, Valve sued <a href="/wiki/Activision_Blizzard" title="Activision Blizzard">Activision Blizzard</a>, which acquired <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Entertainment" title="Sierra Entertainment">Sierra Entertainment</a> after a merger with its parent company, <a href="/wiki/Vivendi_Universal_Games" class="mw-redirect" title="Vivendi Universal Games">Vivendi Universal Games</a>. Activision had allegedly refused to honor the <i>Valve v. Vivendi</i> arbitration agreement. Activision had only paid Valve $1,967,796 of the $2,391,932 award, refusing to pay the remaining $424,136, claiming it had overpaid that sum in the past years.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dota_intellectual_property_ownership"><i>Dota</i> intellectual property ownership</span></h3>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Defense_of_the_Ancients" title="Defense of the Ancients">Defense of the Ancients</a></i> (DotA) was a landmark <a href="/wiki/Mod_(video_gaming)" title="Mod (video gaming)">mod</a> first released in 2003 that created the basis of the genre of <a href="/wiki/Multiplayer_online_battle_arena" title="Multiplayer online battle arena">multiplayer online battle arena</a> (MOBA). It was originally developed by "Eul" within <a href="/wiki/Blizzard_Entertainment" title="Blizzard Entertainment">Blizzard Entertainment</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Warcraft_III:_Reign_of_Chaos" title="Warcraft III: Reign of Chaos">Warcraft III: Reign of Chaos</a></i> via its <a href="/wiki/Warcraft_III_World_Editor" class="mw-redirect" title="Warcraft III World Editor">world editor</a>, and spawned several similar efforts, notably <i>DotA-Allstars</i>. While there had been several that contributed to <i>DotA-Allstars</i>, the project was managed primarily by <a href="/wiki/Steve_%22Guinsoo%22_Feak" class="mw-redirect" title="Steve &quot;Guinsoo&quot; Feak">Steve "Guinsoo" Feak</a>, and later by "<a href="/wiki/IceFrog" title="IceFrog">IceFrog</a>". IceFrog was eventually hired by Valve in 2009, with the rights to the <i>DotA</i> <a href="/wiki/Intellectual_property" title="Intellectual property">intellectual property</a> being sold to Valve the following year. Eul was also hired into Valve by 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-dota_ip_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dota_ip-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Valve then subsequently filed <a href="/wiki/Trademark" title="Trademark">trademarks</a> towards a sequel to <i>DotA</i>, titled <i><a href="/wiki/Dota_2" title="Dota 2">Dota 2</a></i>. DotA-Allstars, LLC, a group of former contributors to the <i>DotA-Allstars</i> project, filed an opposing trademark in August 2010 to contest Valve's claim it owned the property rights.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>DotA-Allstars, LLC was eventually acquired by Blizzard to start development of <i>Blizzard All-Stars</i>. Blizzard took over the trademark challenge. The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Patent_%26_Trademark_Office" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Patent &amp; Trademark Office">United States Patent &amp; Trademark Office</a> initially ruled in Valve's favor. By this point, <a href="/wiki/Riot_Games" title="Riot Games">Riot Games</a> had hired Guinsoo to help develop their own MOBA, <i><a href="/wiki/League_of_Legends" title="League of Legends">League of Legends</a></i>. As with IceFrog, Feak transferred his rights to the <i>Dota</i> property to Riot, who in turn sold those to Blizzard. Blizzard filed a lawsuit against Valve to challenge Valve's ownership, pitting the rights assigned through IceFrog to Guinsoo at odds.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> The case <i>Blizzard Entertainment v. Valve Corporation</i> was settled out of court in May 2012; Valve retained the right to use <i>Dota</i> commercially, while Blizzard reserved the right for fans to use <i>Dota</i> non-commercially.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> Blizzard changed the names of its own projects to remove the <i>Dota</i> term, and renamed <i>Blizzard All-Stars</i> as <i><a href="/wiki/Heroes_of_the_Storm" title="Heroes of the Storm">Heroes of the Storm</a></i>. Valve's <i>Dota 2</i> was released in 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2014, mobile developers Lilith and uCool released their games <i>Dota Legends</i> and <i>Heroes Charge</i>, respectively. Both were influenced by <i>Dota</i> and the sequels. In 2017, Valve and Blizzard took joint action against these companies, citing copyright issues related to the <i>Dota</i> names. uCool argued that the <i>Dota</i> games were a <a href="/wiki/Collective_work_(US)" title="Collective work (US)">collective work</a> and could not be copyrighted by anyone in particular, but the presiding judge, <a href="/wiki/Charles_R._Breyer" class="mw-redirect" title="Charles R. Breyer">Charles R. Breyer</a>, felt that due to the trio's actions as maintainers of the <i>Dota</i> mods, that they rightful have copyright claim to this. Separately, Lilith and uCool argued that Eul had, in a forum post dated September 2004, assigned an <a href="/wiki/Open-source_software" title="Open-source software">open-source copyright license</a> to <i>Dota</i>, which would make Valve and Blizzard's copyright claims void. The case is scheduled to be heard by a jury to resolve this matter at a later date.<sup id="cite_ref-dota_ip_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dota_ip-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="ACCC_v._Valve_Corporation"><i>ACCC v. Valve Corporation</i></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Australian_Competition_and_Consumer_Commission" title="Australian Competition and Consumer Commission">Australian Competition and Consumer Commission</a> (ACCC) announced it was taking action against Valve in 2014. On March 29, 2016, Valve was found guilty of breaching Australian consumer law because:<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Valve claimed consumers were not entitled to a refund for digitally downloaded games purchased from Valve via the Steam website or Steam Client (in any circumstances);</li>
<li>Valve had excluded statutory guarantees and/or warranties that goods would be of acceptable quality; and</li>
<li>Valve had restricted or modified statutory guarantees and/or warranties of acceptable quality.</li></ul>
<p>During the prosecution of this case, Valve implemented a refund policy for Steam purchases, but the case still reviewed Valve's actions prior to the onset of the lawsuit. The court overseeing the case sided with the ACCC in assigning a <span style="white-space: nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Australian_dollar" title="Australian dollar">A$</a>3&#160;million</span> (about <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$2.1&#160;million</span>) fine against Valve in December 2016, as well as requiring Valve to inform Australian consumers of their rights when purchasing games from Steam.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Valve appealed the court's determination that it "engaged in misleading or deceptive conduct and made false or misleading representations about consumer guarantees", as well as seeking to appeal the fine, but the Australian higher courts rejected the appeals in December 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> In January 2018, Valve filed for a "special leave" of the court's decision, appealing to the <a href="/wiki/High_Court_of_Australia" title="High Court of Australia">High Court of Australia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> The High Court dismissed this claim in April 2018, asserting that Valve still was liable under Australian law since it sold products directly to its citizens.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="UFC_Que_Choisir_v._Valve_Corporation"><i>UFC Que Choisir v. Valve Corporation</i></span></h3>
<p>Consumer rights group <a href="/wiki/UFC_Que_Choisir" title="UFC Que Choisir">UFC Que Choisir</a>, based in France, filed a lawsuit against Valve in December 2015, claiming users should be able to resell their software.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="A.M._v._Valve_Corporation"><i>A.M. v. Valve Corporation</i></span></h3>
<p>A former employee filed a $3.1 million lawsuit in May 2016 alleging mistreatment after sex reassignment surgery, and that Valve was exploiting workers.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Skins_gambling">Skins gambling</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Skin_gambling" title="Skin gambling">Skin gambling</a></div>
<p>Valve was named as a defendant in two lawsuits in June and July 2016 related to third-party gambling sites that use the Steamworks API to allow betting with the virtual currency of cosmetic weapon replacement textures, better known as "skins", from <i><a href="/wiki/Counter-Strike:_Global_Offensive" title="Counter-Strike: Global Offensive">Counter-Strike: Global Offensive</a></i>, which through these sites can be converted from or to real-world money. Both suits assert Valve aiding in underaged <a href="/wiki/Gambling" title="Gambling">gambling</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-polygon_gambling_overview_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-polygon_gambling_overview-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> Valve subsequently stated it has no commercial ties with these sites, and that it would demand these sites cease their use of the Steamworks API as they violate the authorized use policies.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> In October 2016, the Washington State Gambling Commission required Valve to stop the use of virtual skins for gambling on Steam, stating they would face legal repercussions if they failed to co-operate.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> On October 17, 2016, Valve sent a letter to the Washington State Gambling Commission stating that they had "no business relationship with such gambling sites", asserting that they come into existence, operate, and go out of existence without their knowledge and consent, adding that they were not aware of any such law that Steam or any of their games were violating.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anti-competitive_practices">Anti-competitive practices</span></h3>
<p>In February 2017, the <a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">European Commission</a> began investigating Valve and five other publishers—<a href="/wiki/Bandai_Namco_Entertainment" title="Bandai Namco Entertainment">Bandai Namco Entertainment</a>, <a href="/wiki/Capcom" title="Capcom">Capcom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Focus_Home_Interactive" title="Focus Home Interactive">Focus Home Interactive</a>, <a href="/wiki/Koch_Media" title="Koch Media">Koch Media</a> and <a href="/wiki/ZeniMax_Media" title="ZeniMax Media">ZeniMax Media</a>—for anti-competitive practices, specifically the use of geo-blocking through the Steam storefront and Steam product keys to prevent access to software to citizens of certain countries. Such practices would be against the <a href="/wiki/Digital_Single_Market" title="Digital Single Market">Digital Single Market</a> initiative by the European Union.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id=".22Valve_Time.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="&quot;Valve_Time&quot;">"Valve Time"</span></h2>
<p><span id="Valve_Time"></span>
</p>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Valve Time" redirects here. For timing of the opening and closing of valves, see <a href="/wiki/Valve_timing" title="Valve timing">Valve timing</a>.</div>
<p>"Valve Time" is an industry term used jokingly with game releases from Valve, used to acknowledge the difference between the "promised" date for released content stated by Valve and to the "actual" release date; "Valve Time" includes predominant delays but also includes some content that was released earlier than expected. Valve itself has fully acknowledged the term, including tracking known discrepancies between ideal and actual releases on their public development wiki<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> and using it in announcements about such delays.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> Valve ascribes delays to their mentality of team-driven initiatives over corporate deadlines to make sure they provide a high-quality product to their customers.<sup id="cite_ref-faliszek_valvetime_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-faliszek_valvetime-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Valve's former business development chief Jason Holtman stated that the company sees themselves as an "oddity" in an industry that looks towards punctual delivery of products; instead, Valve "[tries] as hard as we can to make the best thing possible in the right time frame and get people content they want to consume. And if that takes longer, that's fine".<sup id="cite_ref-valvetime_Holtman_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-valvetime_Holtman-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> For that, Valve takes the concept of "Valve Time" as a compliment, and that "having customers consistently looking at our property or something you've done and saying, can you give me more" is evidence that they are making the right decisions with their game releases, according to Holtman.<sup id="cite_ref-valvetime_Holtman_121-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-valvetime_Holtman-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> The company does try to avoid unintentional delays of their projects,<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> and believes that the earlier occurrences of "Valve Time" delays, primarily from <i>Half-Life</i> development, has helped them improve their release schedules.<sup id="cite_ref-faliszek_valvetime_120-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-faliszek_valvetime-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-76"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-76">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20130831062321/http://pipeline.valvesoftware.com/about_us.php">"Pipeline&#160;— About Us"</a>. <li id="cite_note-pipeline_home-77"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-pipeline_home_77-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-pipeline_home_77-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-pipeline_home_77-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20140820163118/http://pipeline.valvesoftware.com/">"Pipeline&#160;— Home"</a>. <i>pipeline.valvesoftware.com</i>. Valve Corporation. Archived from <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://pipeline.valvesoftware.com/">the original</a> on August 20, 2014<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">August 31,</span> 2013</span>. <q><b>Why is Valve doing this?</b> There are two main reasons that Valve is creating Pipeline. The first is that we are frequently asked questions by teenagers about the videogame industry. "What is it like to work on videogames? What should I study? What colleges are best for preparing me? How do I get a job in videogames?" Pipeline will be a place where those questions can be discussed. The second is that Valve is running an experiment. Traditionally Valve has been a very good place for very experienced videogame developers, and not so good at teaching people straight out of school (the reasons for this and the tradeoffs are covered in the Valve employee handbook). Pipeline is an experiment to see if we can take a group of high school students with minimal work experience and train them in the skills and methods necessary to be successful at a company like Valve.<br /><b>What is the objective of the website itself?</b> We want to establish a connection to the world of teenagers that are asking many questions about getting into the gaming industry. We look to answer many of these questions and are willing to reach out to the community and give them the information they need.<br /><b>Why Pipeline?</b> The name 'Pipeline' was chosen for its industrial connection with names like Valve and Steam, as well as its definition's notion of connection, direction, outreach, and supply. Valve is often asked questions such as "how do I get from where I am now to working at a professional level in a video game company like Valve?" Naturally, like a pipeline, the journey is long, but the goal of this project is to act as a direct feed of knowledge from Valve to the community in order to help equip individuals with the skills necessary to achieve their goals of getting into the video game industry. We will supply information on a variety of different fields prompted by public demand. Information will come in the form of interviews with professionals and resources on this website, displaying the variety of ways that you can achieve your goals.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=pipeline.valvesoftware.com&amp;rft.atitle=Pipeline+%E2%80%94+Home&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fpipeline.valvesoftware.com%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AValve+Corporation" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/></span>
</li>

Retrieved <span class="nowrap">May 1,</span> 2009</span>. <q>Against that backdrop, Activision cut Valve a check last week for $1,967,796—the amount handed down by the arbitrator less the disputed $424K. According to Valve's suit, Activision said that it wouldn't pay the rest and if Valve went to court Activision would countersue. Valve has apparently called Activision's bluff and the parties are now once again at odds.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=It%27s+Ugly%3A+Valve+Sues+Activision%2C+Activision+Threatens+to+Sue+Valve&amp;rft.pub=gamepolitics.com&amp;rft.date=2009-04-30&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.gamepolitics.com%2F2009%2F04%2F30%2Fit039s-ugly-valve-sues-activision-activision-threatens-sue-valve&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AValve+Corporation" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/></span>
